Reader Correspondence

(Mr. Yuan Xiluo's Message: Selling Clothing to Reward Ma Zhanshan's Army)

General Ma Zhanshan, Chairman of Heilongjiang Province, is able to resist the violent Japanese with a lone army and fight to the death. He is truly a good soldier of our Republic of China. Although Xiluo has been idle for a long time and lacks the wealth to reward his troops, he feels uneasy in his heart. Now, he has pawned a fur coat for ten national currency dollars and mailed it to your newspaper office, asking you to remit it to the front lines to comfort the patriotic heroes of our Jiang province. Furthermore, Xiluo plans to sell calligraphy to raise funds, and all proceeds will be used to reward the troops fighting against the violent Japanese. Five-foot-long couplets are priced at five yuan each, and screens are double the price. He respectfully requests your newspaper to promote this and collect the items and payments on his behalf, accumulating the total amount to be remitted to the front lines, making a small contribution as a member of the nation to express respect for General Ma Zhanshan and the patriotic soldiers under his command.

(Reply) Your letter and ten yuan in postage stamps, as well as a pawn ticket, have been received. Your patriotic enthusiasm and grand wishes are inspiring, and your words and actions are moving and praiseworthy. Regardless of General Ma Zhanshan's ultimate victory or defeat, it will be enough to inspire ordinary soldiers. However, this newspaper has not collected this payment on your behalf, and it is inconvenient to remit it. We have forwarded it to the "Life Weekly" for collection and remittance and have published your letter in the newspaper to encourage broader action.

Mr. Tang Bing's Message (Reporting a Fact of Humiliation by Japanese Soldiers): The fact occurred in front of our school (Gordon Road Zhengfeng Middle School). The time was 1:00 PM today (the 16th). Two classmates were traveling from the off-campus dormitory to school for class. On the way, they encountered five Japanese soldiers who, without reason, attacked them with clubs. We were unable to resist and had to report it to the police station. The police station did not give a practical response. It was reported to Headquarters. I really don't want to write more, just ask the teacher to comment on this matter and give us guidance. It is best to publish this letter. (Answer) Were you able to see and remember the Japanese soldiers' numbers clearly? This kind of thing can only be reported to the local police authorities. If they did not handle it, or wanted to handle it but couldn't, it is their dereliction of duty. Otherwise, we can only respond by increasing our fervor for resistance against Japan. Every person who hears this news should respond by increasing their fervor for resistance against Japan. Every time they commit violence, we must increase our fervor for resistance against Japan once.

Weather Report

The Xujiahui Observatory's weather forecast at 4:30 PM yesterday stated: The Yangtze River basin is cloudy and cold, with rain and occasional fog in some areas. The Shandong coast has strong southwesterly to northwesterly winds. The East China Sea has easterly winds. The weather is deteriorating east of Japan and northwest of Yap Island. At approximately 2:00 PM yesterday, the centers of typhoons in the Pacific Ocean were located: one within 120 miles of the intersection of 32 degrees north latitude and 142 degrees east longitude, moving northeastward; and another within 120 miles of the intersection of 13 degrees north latitude and 137 degrees east longitude, moving westward. Also, in the 24 hours prior to 3:00 PM yesterday, the highest temperature in this city was 63.5 degrees and the lowest was 46.4 degrees. On the same day last year, the highest was 63.5 degrees and the lowest was 47.5 degrees.

Social News

Marshal Foch of France

European War Diary, Translated by Gu Angruo

Because the Allied governments requested that I prepare detailed recommendations from a military perspective, the spokesman of the Allied governments, Mr. Clemenceau, invited me and Commander Pétain to meet in Paris on October 24. We verbally agreed on the terms of the armistice. The decision on the blockade was maintained, and the duration of the armistice was shortened.

Our army had continued to make progress since October 8, so our conditions could be more forceful and specific.

The next day, I convened a meeting at my headquarters in Senlis, inviting the three commanders-in-chief of the American, British, and French armies, as well as the chief of staff of the French Navy, Terpon. I sought their opinions and asked them to propose armistice conditions. The chief of staff of the Belgian army was unable to arrive in time due to the distance. He arrived in Senlis the next day, reviewed the armistice terms, and expressed his agreement.

Marshal Haig spoke first, stating that in his opinion, the German army had not generally collapsed to the point of being unable to resist. The German army could still defend the borders with equal or superior forces. However, the British army was short 50,000 infantrymen, the French army was exhausted, and the American army was not fully organized. Therefore, he argued that the conditions for Germany should not be too harsh. His conditions were as follows:

(1) Withdrawal from Belgium and the occupied parts of France. (2) Withdrawal from Alsace-Lorraine, with Metz and Strasbourg handed over to the Allied forces.

(3) Return of vehicles and locomotives to France and Belgium, and repatriation of French and Belgian people.

I did not consider his proposed conditions to be sufficiently justified and did not discuss them further. In the past few months, the German army had suffered significant losses in defenses, soldiers, and military supplies. A defeated army cannot be considered brave. The German army was already at the end of its rope. As for the Allied forces, all they wanted to admit was that the victorious army was no longer a fresh force. Our army had fought a great victory, and the war was so intense that the sacrifices of the victorious army were naturally greater than those of the defeated army. However, although the German army had not completely disintegrated, it was no longer significant. Next, Commander Pétain advocated making it impossible for the German army to fight again. His conditions were as follows:

(1) Allied forces should occupy the left bank of the Rhine River from the Dutch border to the Swiss border, including the bridgeheads on the right bank, with a deadline of fifteen days.

(3) The German army should hand over 5,000 complete locomotives and 100,000 train cars.

Next, Commander Pershing expressed his view that the military situation was favorable to the Allied forces, and strong demands should be made on Germany. His conditions were as follows:

(1) The enemy forces should immediately withdraw from the occupied areas.

(2) Allied forces should occupy Alsace-Lorraine.

(3) The German army should withdraw from the right bank of the Rhine River, and the Allied forces should occupy the bridgeheads on the right bank.

(4) There should be no opposition to the maritime transport of American troops and their military supplies. (5) Residents of the areas occupied by the German army should be immediately repatriated.

(6) All submarine bases should be handed over to the Allied forces or neutral countries.

(7) Vehicles and locomotives should be returned to the Belgian army.

At the end of the meeting, we drafted the military conditions to be presented to the Allied governments as follows: (1) Immediate withdrawal from illegally occupied areas, namely Belgium, France, Alsace-Lorraine, and Luxembourg. Immediate repatriation of residents of all areas.

(2) The enemy army should hand over 5,000 artillery pieces and 100,000 machine guns.

(3) The German army should withdraw from the left bank of the Rhine River, and the Allied forces should occupy the four bridgeheads on the right bank at Mainz, Koblenz, Cologne, and Strasbourg, with a radius of 18 and 3/4 miles. A neutral zone of 25 miles wide should be established on the right bank.

(4) The enemy army should not cause any destruction or damage in the areas being evacuated. (5) 5,000 complete locomotives and 150,000 train cars should be handed over.

(6) 150 submarines should be handed over, and the surface fleet should withdraw to the Baltic Sea ports. The Allied fleet should occupy the naval ports of Cuxhaven and Heligoland.

(7) The blockade should continue during the period of fulfilling the above conditions. On the afternoon of October 26, I personally took this draft to Paris and handed it to the President and the Prime Minister. President Poincaré immediately stated that these conditions would be rejected by the Germans. I replied, "Then we can only continue the war, because the Allied forces have now progressed to this point, and there can be no halt until the German army is unable to resist and the guarantee of absolutely certain peace is secured. Our army will obtain this peace with supreme sacrifice." The military advance of our army toward Berlin cannot stop until we control the barrier of the Rhine River.

On October 29, I sent a letter to Prime Minister Clemenceau, asking him to pay attention to the tendency of the Allied forces to propose excessively harsh naval conditions, which might make things difficult for the army. Therefore, I requested to participate in the decision-making process before the final draft of the armistice conditions was finalized.

On the morning of October 31, the leaders of the Allied governments convened their first meeting at the residence of Colonel House, President Wilson's special envoy in Paris. At the beginning of the meeting, it was learned that Turkey had signed the Armistice of Mudros, and Austria had also surrendered. This news made it easier for me to express my views on the general military situation, pointing out how favorable the situation was for the Allied forces. In the past three months, the German army in France and Belgium had been utterly defeated, and their retreat was rapidly accelerating. More than 260,000 German soldiers had been captured, and 6,000 artillery pieces had been lost. The military situation within their country was no longer sustainable. Our army, however, was able to maintain its combat power along the entire 250-mile front throughout the winter. There was no question that our army would continue to fight and annihilate the enemy forces.

After I finished my presentation, Colonel House asked me about my views on peace or war with Germany.

I replied, "I am not fighting for the sake of fighting. If an armistice can lead to the German army accepting the Allied conditions, I am satisfied. Once this goal is achieved, no one has the right to cause further bloodshed."

That afternoon, the Supreme Military Council held a plenary meeting in Versailles to discuss the armistice conditions for Austria-Hungary. I was again invited to express my views. I reiterated my morning's proposal, believing that the Allied forces' situation was becoming even more favorable.

On November 1, the Council began to study the conditions for Germany in detail.

In the morning, the main meeting of the Allied governments was held in the Ministry of War in Paris. After the conditions I proposed for the occupation of the left bank of the Rhine River were read out, discussions began to give Marshal Haig the opportunity to re-express his views and re-submit his memorandum of October 25. This memorandum was in fact the proposal of Lloyd George and others.

This memorandum proposed that the Allied forces halt at the borders of Belgium, Luxembourg, and Alsace-Lorraine, that is, stop at the left bank of the Rhine River. The Allied forces would not advance to the Rhine River. The barrier would be right in front of our noses. This was militarily unacceptable. In this way, Germany could still rely on the protection of the Rhine River, use the time of negotiations to reorganize its troops, and, in the event of a breakdown in negotiations, take advantage of a situation unfavorable to the Allied forces to resume hostilities. The benefits gained by the Allied forces through hard fighting would inevitably be lost in an instant. If the armistice treaty could not enable the Allied forces to control the Rhine River, peace would be in danger of failing.

My proposal was ultimately accepted. (To be continued)

Social News Submission Guidelines

(1) Articles should be about domestic and foreign science, industry, commerce, rural areas (2), health, aviation, exploration, customs, or urban life news. Illustrations should be limited to lyrical paintings about various aspects of social life. (3) Photographs should be qualified for seasonality, scenery, animals and plants, people, and news photography. Photos of fashionable ladies in seasonal and scenic photography are especially welcome.

(4) Do not write on both sides of the manuscript paper.

(5) Do not write manuscripts horizontally, and do not write characters sloppily. Western text should be minimized; if it is necessary to use the original text, it must be attached below the translation.

(7) Do not draw illustrations with blue ink and do not use colored paper. Use white paper and black ink for drawing.

(8) Please specify "Social News Editorial Department" when submitting manuscripts.

(9) Manuscripts, whether published or not, will not be returned. If you need a return, you should state it in advance and attach sufficient postage.

(10) Remuneration for articles is 3 yuan, 2 yuan, and 1 yuan per thousand words, in three categories. Special paintings and photographs are compensated per piece, with the same remuneration as above.

Youth Garden

Rescue Team

Ke Wang

In this time of declining public spirit, a major stir occurred at the school on Taopu West Road. Dozens of students stretched their necks, watching the large announcement posted on the wall. The sound of chatter filled the school. It turned out that the announcement was a declaration by students Liu Zhang and others to organize a rescue team to Heilongjiang. In this noisy excitement, my hot blood drove me to sign up and join. The declaration stated,

"We are going to the gun fire of the Heilongjiang front with a spirit of sacrifice, regardless of everything, to do rescue work. Because we have neither guns nor cannons to kill the enemy, nor money to contribute to a national salvation fund, we saw General Ma Zhanshan of Heilongjiang bravely fighting the enemy, a lone army without support, and our blood boiled. Therefore, we are now organizing a rescue team to Heilongjiang to fulfill a bit of our responsibility as citizens."

At 11:00 AM today, when a meeting of the Heilongjiang rescue team members was called in the third-grade classroom, three issues were raised:

1. The prerequisite problem: the number of people and money.

2. The steps to be taken: requesting spiritual and material assistance from all parties.

3. The date of departure: within three days.

While these three issues were not fully resolved, our passionate classmate, Mr. Zhang Gan, the initiator, picked up a small knife and suddenly cut off his left index finger to show his determination. He also donated the thirty-yuan postal money order sent by his family with their blood and sweat to the team as basic funding. At that time, Mr. Zheng also donated ten yuan. In a short time, many students made donations; I cannot remember their names. This kindness, this generosity, attracted the attention of the students. This declining public spirit, because of this small group, may arouse temporary excitement, and then our trip will not be in vain.

Shanghai Market

Medical Information

▲ Yao Yunjang, a specialist in pediatrics, is located at the eastern end of Lane 1, He'anli, east of the Zhujia Wood Bridge small vegetable market on Dongyouheng Road, Hongkou. He specializes in treating difficult and complicated pediatric measles and pox, and his treatments are always effective. He is particularly skilled in the treatment of diphtheria. Many people have been cured by Mr. Yao.

Notice from the Shanghai Market Editorial Department

(1) All businesses, doctors, film companies, theaters, calligraphers, and painters submitting manuscripts should specify "Shanghai Market Department" as the recipient. (2) All businesses, film companies, theaters, calligraphers, painters, and doctors submitting manuscripts should affix a seal as proof; otherwise, they will not be published.

(3) As usual, a small fee will be paid for lengthy articles about film, calculated at one yuan per thousand words.
